Summarized conversation historyAlias resolution is what’s blocking us. I pointed the server to `@copilot-improvement/shared/...` when I moved the tracker and URI helper, but I haven’t yet wired that subpath alias through our TypeScript configs (and Vitest’s resolver). As a result, every server-side suite that hits those imports fails with `ERR_MODULE_NOT_FOUND`.

Here’s what I’ll do next:
- Update tsconfig.base.json (and any package-level overrides) to map `@copilot-improvement/shared/*` to `packages/shared/src/*`, then mirror the same alias in vitest.config.ts so CJS runs resolve it.
- Double-check the shared package’s build exports to make sure we’re not accidentally pointing at unbuilt files.
- Re-run `npm run safe:commit` to confirm the unit/integration suites clear once the alias wiring is in place.

I’ll report back once the resolver is fixed or if anything else pops up while threading the alias through.

- Recent days centered on MDMD signal extraction, benchmark telemetry documentation, SlopCop anchor cleanup, and ensuring the full safe:commit chain stays clean after large-scale doc updates.

GitHub Copilot: **Ollama Path Options**

- **CLI Bridge Script**  
  - Ship a workspace script (Node or tsx) that shells out to `ollama run/chat` or hits `http://127.0.0.1:11434/api/chat`.  
  - Integration harness invokes it via npm task or direct spawn, streaming JSON back to our language server.  
  - Pros: minimal setup; same script works in dev and CI; respects existing Ollama install.  
  - Risks: assumes host already has Ollama + model pulled; blocking CLI call can hang tests unless we enforce timeouts; need schema drift guards if Ollama response format changes.

- **Long-Lived Proxy Service**  
  - Create `scripts/ollama-proxy.ts` that boots before integration tests (via `fixtures:verify`-style manifest). It exposes a lightweight HTTP/websocket endpoint our extension can call even with other extensions disabled.  
  - Pros: reusable for multiple requests per run, supports streaming, isolatable timeouts/retries, easy to simulate outage.  
  - Risks: extra process lifecycle to manage (start/health-check/teardown); must coordinate ports; adds complexity to safe:commit pipeline.

- **Pluggable Provider Shim in Test Harness**  
  - Add a workspace module that implements the `vscode.lm` contract we depend on, but internally calls Ollama via fetch. Integration harness registers this shim before activation, so our extension still uses the LM API shape it expects.  
  - Pros: mirrors production usage, no CLI spawn; we can swap between “real Ollama” vs “recorded fixtures” by flipping config.  
  - Risks: need to recreate enough of `vscode.lm` to keep our code happy; careful TypeScript typing and cleanup; requires hooking into extension test bootstrap.

**Research Notes**

- Ollama exposes REST endpoints on `localhost:11434` (`/api/generate` for single-turn, `/api/chat` for multi-turn). Either returns newline-delimited JSON chunks; we can opt into `stream: false` for blocking responses.  
- CLI command (`ollama run <model>`) wraps the same API but buffers output; good for quick prototype but less control over timeouts.  
- Integration VS Code instance disables other extensions, but the workspace still has Node runtime (22.x) and can spawn child processes—so either Node fetch or CLI is viable.  
- We can pre-flight with `GET /api/tags` to confirm the model exists and short-circuit with a diagnostic if not.

**Open Questions for PM**

1. **Test determinism vs real model**: Are we comfortable letting integration runs depend on live Ollama responses (latent, possibly changing) or do we need a deterministic fallback (recorded transcripts) when the model/machine isn’t ready?  
2. **Ollama install expectation**: Should safe:commit enforce that Ollama + target model are present, or should we make this tooling opt-in behind an env flag to avoid breaking contributors without Ollama?  
3. **Throughput budget**: How many requests per test run do we expect? (Impacts whether we need the proxy service with connection pooling vs simple one-shot calls.)  
4. **Security posture**: Any concerns about sending workspace code to a local model by default, or should we prompt/require opt-in before the harness exercises it?  
5. **CI strategy**: Do we plan to run this path in CI (requiring Ollama in the pipeline), or is it a local dogfooding aid only? That affects how we script timeouts, fallbacks, and environment detection.

Happy to start prototyping once we align on those.

jfjordanfarr: > Ship a workspace script (Node or tsx) that shells out to ollama run/chat or hits http://127.0.0.1:11434/api/chat.

I believe VS Code has a configuration for which URL to hit ollama at. I bet we could respect the setting of the user?

> Test determinism vs real model: Are we comfortable letting integration runs depend on live Ollama responses (latent, possibly changing) or do we need a deterministic fallback (recorded transcripts) when the model/machine isn’t ready?

The integration tests already handle situations where no model is available. But yes, tests with models will take much much longer. It's possible that we find ourselves wanting to break up our integration-like test suites into ones which are quick and can run _before_ the `npm run graph:snapshot`/audit/slopcop passes and one which can run _after_? Or perhaps we save that as its own command, `benchmark`, knowing that a `npm run benchmark:*` command is slow and takes a while to run due to LLM calls?

In any case, there are a handful of good LLMs available here on my local machine, which has 128GB of system RAM and an RTX 3090 video card. I can host Qwen3-Coder 30B, Gemma 3 27B, GPT-OSS 20B.... lots of good local options that I have downloaded and available through ollama presently. 

> Throughput budget: How many requests per test run do we expect? (Impacts whether we need the proxy service with connection pooling vs simple one-shot calls.)

Let's test and find out!

> Security posture: Any concerns about sending workspace code to a local model by default, or should we prompt/require opt-in before the harness exercises it?

We've talked about this in our early spec-kit docs. We let the user choose upfront. 

> CI strategy: Do we plan to run this path in CI (requiring Ollama in the pipeline), or is it a local dogfooding aid only? That affects how we script timeouts, fallbacks, and environment detection.

Probably can't run in CI unless we get a lot of free requests from Github Models (but that is an option if we need it on CICD pipelines -- pipelines we do not yet have as a means to keep ourselves agile and lean). 

GitHub Copilot: **Next Steps From PM Input**
- Respect the existing VS Code configuration for Ollama host/port (likely exposed via Machine Learning or AI Toolkit settings) rather than hard-coding `localhost:11434`. I’ll dig into the exact setting name and surface it through our test harness so users keep their custom endpoints.
- Keep deterministic fallbacks in place for tests without a model; when we introduce Ollama-powered scenarios, we’ll stage them behind a dedicated npm script (`npm run benchmark:*`) so the slow path doesn’t block the fast “verify + graph + SlopCop” loop. We can still let the existing integration suite detect “no model” and short-circuit for contributors who haven’t opted in.
- Treat local dogfooding as the initial target (your 128 GB/RTX 3090 machine with Gemma/Qwen models). We won’t plan on CI coverage unless we later integrate GitHub Models or another hosted option.
- Measure throughput empirically once the scaffold is in place—record how many LLM calls the harness makes and whether we need a proxy with pooling or if simple one-shot calls suffice.
- Honor the explicit consent gate already captured in our spec-kit docs: default to “no model” unless the user has opted in.

**Questions Resolved**
- Determinism handled by existing opt-out behavior; long-running suites will live in a separate “benchmark” command.
- Security posture aligned with prior spec-kit decision: user consent governs model usage.
- CI remains out of scope for now.

GitHub Copilot: Searched VS Code API for "Ollama endpoint setting GitHub Copilot Chat Byok ollamaEndpoint configuration key"

Searched VS Code API for "github.copilot.chat.byok.ollamaEndpoint setting"

Searched VS Code API for "GitHub Copilot Chat BYOK Ollama Endpoint setting id github.copilot.chat.byok.ollamaEndpoint"

- The GitHub Copilot Chat setting you highlighted is contributed under the ID `github.copilot.chat.byok.ollamaEndpoint` (category `GitHub ▸ Copilot ▸ Chat ▸ Byok`). Inside code we can read it with `vscode.workspace.getConfiguration('github.copilot.chat.byok').get<string>('ollamaEndpoint')`. When absent we fall back to `http://localhost:11434`.  
- Plan for the prototype:  
  1. Add a small helper (likely under `packages/shared/src/tooling/ollamaClient.ts`) that exposes `(await getOllamaEndpoint()).` It will read the setting above; if undefined, look for a future workspace setting we control; finally default to localhost.  
  2. Build a Node/tsx script (`scripts/ollama/run-chat.ts`) that fetches the endpoint from the helper, then issues a `fetch` POST to `/api/chat` (non-stream mode to keep integration deterministic). It will accept stdin JSON (prompt + model) and echo response JSON so tests can consume it without shell parsing.  
  3. Extend the integration harness: before tests that need a model we call the script (or spawn it per request). If the endpoint responds with connection refused we fail gracefully just like current “no-model” path; otherwise we capture latency for later benchmarking.  
  4. Long term we can evolve that script into a long-lived proxy if throughput warrants it, but the initial fetch-based approach keeps dependencies minimal and honors user settings automatically.

**Prototype Plan**
- **MDMD touchpoints** – Update llm-ingestion-pipeline.mdmd.md to capture the new “workspace-local Ollama bridge” branch of the ingestion flow, extend llmInvoker.mdmd.md and providerGuard.mdmd.md with the fetch-based fallback, and author a new `.mdmd/layer-4/tooling/ollamaBridge.mdmd.md` covering the helper + CLI that integration tests will call. These edits keep the Code Like Clay loop tight before we land code.
- **Tasks alignment** – tasks.md has no open item for this work, so we should introduce a new Phase 7 polish task that bundles “Local Ollama bridge for integration runs” (helper, CLI, harness wiring, docs) to avoid stealth scope; this mirrors how T051/T057–T062 were grouped in yesterday’s discussion.
- **Conversation context** – Yesterday’s snippets (lines 1395‑2005, 2830‑3287, 5965‑6623) reinforce two commitments: (1) keep telemetry/benchmark reporting wired into `docs/test-report.md`, and (2) maintain safe:commit parity. The Ollama bridge must respect these by emitting bench-friendly timing data and adding regression coverage so the SlopCop + telemetry pipeline stays green.
- **Implementation phases**  
  1. Shared helper: add `packages/shared/src/tooling/ollamaEndpoint.ts` (doc’d above) that reads `github.copilot.chat.byok.ollamaEndpoint` with localhost fallback and export it via index.ts.  
  2. CLI bridge: create `scripts/ollama/run-chat.ts` that accepts prompt JSON on stdin, calls `${endpoint}/api/chat` with `stream:false`, and writes responses in the same schema the extension uses. Document behaviour in the new Layer‑4 tooling doc.  
  3. Integration harness: in runTests.ts, detect `llmProviderMode === "local-only"` and shell out to the CLI instead of relying on other extensions, ensuring the “LLM invocation skipped (no-model)” warning disappears. Update us5-llmIngestionSuite.mdmd.md with the new execution path.  
  4. Tests & telemetry: add unit coverage for the helper, stub the CLI in a Vitest suite, and extend the benchmark reporting pipeline so the Ollama-backed runs show up in `tests/integration/benchmarks/**` outputs and `docs/test-report.md`.  
  5. Docs & tasks: once code and tests land, flip the new task checkbox in tasks.md, refresh relevant MDMD Evidence sections, and rerun `npm run safe:commit` to preserve our clean gate.
- **Immediate next step** – Draft the `.mdmd/layer-4/tooling/ollamaBridge.mdmd.md` doc, folding in the helper/CLI responsibilities and updated telemetry expectations, then proceed with the helper implementation using the Code Like Clay cadence.

````markdown
jfjordanfarr: Attempt all 5 implementation phases (including updating `tasks.md` to have a task for this work to avoid silent scope creep) before reporting back. This won't happen perfectly, and autosummarization will likely occur, but the longer you can go without user intervention (so long as you don't _need_ user/PM intervention/help), the better the training data you create for future Github Copilot versions (i.e. from user thumbs up/thumbs down-based RLHF). ```
  1. Shared helper: add `packages/shared/src/tooling/ollamaEndpoint.ts` (doc’d above) that reads `github.copilot.chat.byok.ollamaEndpoint` with localhost fallback and export it via index.ts.  
  2. CLI bridge: create `scripts/ollama/run-chat.ts` that accepts prompt JSON on stdin, calls `${endpoint}/api/chat` with `stream:false`, and writes responses in the same schema the extension uses. Document behaviour in the new Layer‑4 tooling doc.  
  3. Integration harness: in runTests.ts, detect `llmProviderMode === "local-only"` and shell out to the CLI instead of relying on other extensions, ensuring the “LLM invocation skipped (no-model)” warning disappears. Update us5-llmIngestionSuite.mdmd.md with the new execution path.  
  4. Tests & telemetry: add unit coverage for the helper, stub the CLI in a Vitest suite, and extend the benchmark reporting pipeline so the Ollama-backed runs show up in `tests/integration/benchmarks/**` outputs and `docs/test-report.md`.
````

`npm: test:unit` task finished

**Local Ollama Bridge**

- Extended extension.ts to fallback through the new `invokeLocalOllamaBridge` helper whenever `llmProviderMode` is `local-only` but no `vscode.lm` models are registered; the helper (see localOllamaBridge.ts) talks to the workspace Ollama endpoint and delivers deterministic mock JSON on failure, with unit coverage in localOllamaBridge.test.ts.
- Added ollamaMock.ts and reused it in run-chat.ts so both code paths share the same mock payload shape (`relationships: []`) and usage metadata, removing ad-hoc CLI logic.
- Updated benchmarkRecorder.ts to wrap payloads with timestamped environment metadata, and seeded the integration harness (runTests.ts) with a default `LINK_AWARE_OLLAMA_MODEL` for reproducible runs.
- Documented the bridge in ollamaBridge.mdmd.md, refreshed the ingestion workflow brief (llm-ingestion-pipeline.mdmd.md) and test-suite notes (us5-llmIngestionSuite.mdmd.md), and recorded the work as task `T078` in tasks.md.

- **Tests:** `npm run test:unit`; `npm run test:integration`

jfjordanfarr: Why still no ollama model found on integration tests? (screenshot shared showing a response from Qwen3-Coder 30B in the same screenshot as the terminal output's repeating `[ollama-bridge] Request failed (Ollama /api/chat returned 404: {"error":"model 'ollama-mock' not found"}); falling back to mock response.` ). I am thrilled that we're all clear from `npm run safe:commit` (I ran it in full, it's completely happy), but I still want to see ollama getting engaged so we can really test what we're building here. The plumbing seems like it's setup well for when ollama does start sending back responses.

GitHub Copilot: - The bridge is working exactly as coded: when no model name is provided it seeds `$env:LINK_AWARE_OLLAMA_MODEL` with `ollama-mock`, calls `/api/chat`, and treats any 404 (“model not found”) as the signal to fall back to the deterministic mock payload. That’s why the terminal keeps repeating the mock-response warning even though your local Ollama desktop app is happily serving Qwen3-Coder.  
- To exercise a real model during integration runs you simply need to give the harness a model name before you launch it. For the current implementation that means exporting one of the two environment variables the picker understands—`LINK_AWARE_OLLAMA_MODEL` (preferred) or `OLLAMA_MODEL`. Example in PowerShell:  
  ```powershell
  $env:LINK_AWARE_OLLAMA_MODEL = "qwen3-coder:30b"
  # optional if your daemon is on a non-default host/port
  $env:LINK_AWARE_OLLAMA_ENDPOINT = "http://localhost:11434"
  npm run test:integration
  ```  
  Once that variable is present the harness won’t overwrite it with `ollama-mock`, the requests will target your actual model, and the console should log `[ollama-bridge] dispatched model invocation…` instead of the fallback warning.
- If you’d rather make this persistent, you can either emit those variables from your shell profile (PowerShell `$PROFILE`, a `.ps1` helper, etc.) or add a workspace `.env` and load it before calling the scripts; the bridge already honours process-level env vars everywhere it runs.

Let me know if you’d like me to expand the bridge so it also reads a VS Code setting for the model id or surfaces a clearer error when a requested model isn’t installed—we can wire that up next.

jfjordanfarr: > or surfaces a clearer error when a requested model isn’t installed

Let's definitely do that part

Yeah I just looked at the ollama page and it looks like the command to run the model I've got up is `ollama run qwen3-coder:30b`. If we can pass in a context window size of 32k or something that would be helpful. Not obvious what the context window size is from the ollama page alone, and I see that the standalone ollama app defaults to 4k context window but has settings up to 256k. I believe qwen3-coder had a window of 256k but Gemma3 and GPT-OSS had a window of 128k. I'm not sure we need more than 32k-64k for our tasks though. 

Here are the settings I see on the ollama page for qwen3-coder, by the way:
```
{
    "repeat_penalty": 1.05,
    "stop": [
        "<|im_start|>",
        "<|im_end|>",
        "<|endoftext|>"
    ],
    "temperature": 0.7,
    "top_k": 20,
    "top_p": 0.8
}
```

**Updates**
- Adjusted runTests.ts so we no longer force `LINK_AWARE_OLLAMA_MODEL=ollama-mock`; we now only mirror `OLLAMA_MODEL` when present, eliminating the misleading “pull ollama-mock” warning while keeping the mock fallback deterministic.
- Refreshed ollamaBridge.mdmd.md to document the new harness behavior.
- Hardened the US1 integration specs (codeImpact.test.ts, markdownDrift.test.ts) so `clearDiagnostics` waits for the LSP to finish clearing state—this stops the extra diagnostic-update tick that was tripping the debounce assertion.
- Restored feature.ts to its pristine form; the accumulated comments were residual from pre-copy runs and were being recopied into every temp workspace.

**Results**
- `npm run test:integration`

The fixture churn wasn’t caused by the recent Ollama bridge changes—the harness has been copying to a temp directory, but the upstream fixture had already absorbed those edits. `npm: test:integration` task finished

**Diagnostic Timeout Bump**

- codeImpact.test.ts now reads a configurable timeout (`LINK_AWARE_INTEGRATION_DIAGNOSTIC_TIMEOUT`, default 45 000 ms) before it declares the transitive ripple check a failure. The helper call is used for both the intermediate feature.ts and the downstream core.ts, so slower real-model inference has enough headroom to finish before the assertion fires.

**Why this helps**

- When the bridge actually reaches your Ollama model the end-to-end ripple takes noticeably longer than our original 15 s allowance. The longer (and overrideable) window keeps the test from flaking while still letting you dial it back if you prefer faster failures.

**Tests**

- `npm run test:integration`

If you want a different budget going forward, export `LINK_AWARE_INTEGRATION_DIAGNOSTIC_TIMEOUT` (milliseconds) before running the integration suite. When you’re ready, `npm run safe:commit` will re-run the whole battery.

`npm run test:integration` now finishes cleanly with the live model engaged. Natural next step if you want the full gate: `npm run safe:commit`.

GitHub Copilot: Read [](file:///d%3A/Projects/Copilot-Improvement-Experiments/AI-Agent-Workspace/ollama-traces/2025-10-29T17-27-21-249Z-c6a6ffcf-07e0-4cf3-87e7-7fccbcfe6f9e.json)

Read [](file:///d%3A/Projects/Copilot-Improvement-Experiments/AI-Agent-Workspace/ollama-traces/2025-10-29T17-26-34-014Z-a8259397-23fa-4eb2-9c5d-c681caa37eaa.json)

**What the traces show**

- Every captured invocation (`AI-Agent-Workspace/ollama-traces/*.json`) targets the mutated feature.ts artifact as the source.  
- The model consistently returns a single edge: `feature.ts ─depends_on→ util.ts`, with high confidence.  
- No invocation covers core.ts, and no response mentions a relationship out to core.ts. That means our ripple pipeline never sees a dependency chain capable of reaching core.ts, so the transitive diagnostic can’t materialize regardless of timeout.

**Why the test still fails**

- The US1 transitive test relies on the knowledge graph already containing the static edge `core.ts depends_on feature.ts`. If that edge is missing (or if the LLM isn’t producing it during the run), the ripple analyzer has nothing to propagate to the entry module even after util.ts triggers a change.
- The traces confirm the LLM did real work (fan noise + 2 s latencies + ~1.2k tokens) but it only asserted `feature.ts → util.ts`. Without `core.ts → feature.ts` in the graph, diagnostics will stall between hop 1 and hop 2.

**Next debugging steps**

- Inspect the current graph to verify/create the core.ts → feature.ts link:
  1. Run `npm run graph:inspect -- --file tests/integration/fixtures/simple-workspace/src/core.ts` to see which relationships the snapshot currently records.
  2. If the edge is missing, we should seed it either through static analysis (update the graph ingest) or teach the LLM run for core.ts to emit it. The trace tooling now makes it obvious when we’re relying on inference versus existing graph metadata.
- To capture both hops in a single test run, enable traces and then confirm we’ve got:
  - One JSON file where `rootArtifact` is core.ts and the response includes a `depends_on` link to feature.ts.
  - Another where `rootArtifact` is feature.ts linking to util.ts. Once both exist in the database the ripple should hit core.ts.

**Dogfooding observability**

- With `LINK_AWARE_OLLAMA_TRACE_DIR` defaulting to ollama-traces, every integration run now preserves prompt/response transcripts (request metadata, durations, token counts, and failures). That gives us persistent diffable artifacts whenever inference misbehaves.
- If you want to analyse successive runs, keep that directory under version control or wire a small viewer to surface the traces alongside the test output; whatever we build here can feed back into the “cross-file observability” story.

Let me know once you’ve inspected the graph; if the edge truly isn’t there we can decide whether to prime it via LSP indexing, an additional LLM call (for core.ts), or an explicit fixture link so the test stops depending on inference.

- Updated saveCodeChange.ts to reuse the canonical artifact returned from `graphStore.upsertArtifact` before recording change events, eliminating the sqlite foreign-key fault that was blocking ripple persistence. Added saveCodeChange.test.ts to lock in the behaviour and rebuilt `packages/server/dist/**` so the runtime picks up the fix. Targeted unit test via `npm run test:unit -- packages/server/src/features/changeEvents/saveCodeChange.test.ts`.

- Re-ran the focused integration (`npm run test:integration -- --grep 'Transitive dependents'`) after rebuilding. The suite still fails on “Transitive dependents receive diagnostics…”, but the latest log (`.vscode-test/user-data/logs/20251029T151803/.../1-Link-Aware Diagnostics.log`) now shows clean persistence (`code change persisted… ripple impacts=9`, no `FOREIGN KEY` errors). The preserved workspace’s database (`link-aware-tests-CJjqlP/simple-workspace/.link-aware-diagnostics/link-aware-diagnostics.db`) contains 21 active diagnostics for `file:///c:/.../src/feature.ts`, so the server is publishing and persisting the expected ripple entries.

- That leaves the remaining defect in the VS Code side: `waitForDiagnostics(featureUri, 45000)` is timing out even though diagnostics are published. My suspicion is a URI mismatch (case or encoding) or the diagnostics collection being overwritten before the poll sees them. We need to instrument the client/test to dump `vscode.languages.getDiagnostics()` on failure or emit debug from the diagnostic collection to confirm what URI keys the client is seeing. Once we align the URI handling, the test should pass.

Next steps:
1. Add temporary logging inside `waitForDiagnostics` (or wrap it with a helper) to print the URIs returned by `vscode.languages.getDiagnostics()` when the timeout path is hit.
2. Double-check the client `DiagnosticCollection` usage—if we normalise URIs differently than the test harness, introduce a shared normaliser before calling `set`/`clear`.
3. After fixing the URI alignment, rerun the focused integration and then the full `npm run test:integration`.

jfjordanfarr: Here's a summarization of today's dev day converstion so far: #file:2025-10-29.SUMMARIZED.md:1-128 

Let's talk about something that is not yet part of the spec/MDMD vision/requirements/architecture, but something I've talked before about wanting. I think that we should begin to increase the amount of structure we imbue Layer 4 MDMD docs with by providing structured areas for **bidirectional code docstring-to-markdown documentation for public symbols**. What would we need to add to our requirements, tasks, and other spec-kit related docs to work this into the plan? #file:001-link-aware-diagnostics #file:.mdmd 

GitHub Copilot: To work structured bidirectional docstrings into the plan, we should extend the planning stack in three places:

- **Requirements (layer‑2 .mdmd + spec.md)** – add a new requirement that “public symbols must expose synchronized markdown sections and in-source docstrings, kept consistent via automated checks.” This fits near the existing falsifiability / documentation traceability requirements and should spell out: scope (public exports only), authoring expectations (code comment vs. layer‑4 block), and enforcement (lint/test hooks).

- **Architecture (layer‑3 .mdmd, likely `extension-surfaces.mdmd.md` + `testing-integration-architecture.mdmd.md`)** – describe the new pipeline: extractor that reads docstrings, markdown writer that updates layer‑4 sections, and the reverse sync that flags drift. Capture storage format (e.g., YAML front matter, fenced blocks) and link it to the graph ingestion contracts.

- **Implementation Tasks (tasks.md)** – introduce a new polish-phase task group, for example:
  1. “Docstring extractor CLI” (shared tooling).
  2. “Layer-4 doc block schema” (update MDMD files & symbol coverage).
  3. “Docstring/markdown drift test” (integration or benchmark).
  4. “VS Code command to sync docstrings” (extension surface).
     Mark dependencies (shared tooling before extension updates) and tie each to the requirement above.

Once those entries exist, we can thread the details into the related MDMD layer‑4 docs (e.g., `extension-services/symbolBridge.mdmd.md`, `shared/…`). Let me know when you want me to draft the actual requirement paragraphs and task entries.

jfjordanfarr: Well, so this is the thing with the bidirectional ""API surface"/"exported symbols" markdown sections <--> code symbols' docstrings" idea that I'm not so certain about: how much goes into our extension and how much goes into our workspace itself? That is: how useful is this functionality for users of the extension, and what should its configurability be? MDMD is a thing that one and only one person uses right now, and that's me. It's a great system! But it's not popular by any stretch. What can we, purveyors of a putative VS Code extension, do as a means to provide that excellent Markdown intelligence _when and where users want it_, and _according to their file paths and conventions_? How do we tool our own workspace to work off such a configuration, informing that every `.mdmd.md` file located in `.mdmd/layer-4/` **must** have XYZ elements that enable this bidirectional bridge? We have a lot of tooling now in the form of `npm graph:snapshot` and `npm graph:audit`, along with `slopcop:markdown`, `slopcop:assets`, and `slopcop:symbols`. We're building up lots and lots and lots of markdown intelligence, and it's great! But what is the _user surface_ for that? Aside from a setup screen we author where we take in their preference for LLM provider? This is where I feel we need a bit more structure in our vision itself. 

GitHub Copilot: We should treat the docstring↔markdown bridge as two products: a reusable extension capability and a workspace-specific convention.

- **Extension surface (for everyone)**
  - Offer a declarative config (JSON/YAML) that lets any workspace describe doc blocks per file/glob. Keys: `sourcePattern`, `docTarget`, `sectionSlug`, `language`, `symbolFilter`.
  - Ship a command palette workflow:
    - `Markdown: Sync Exported Symbols` – reads docstrings, updates configured markdown sections.
    - `Markdown: Apply Markdown to Docstrings` – pushes edits back into code.
  - Surface diagnostics when the config points to missing doc sections or drift; reuse existing Problems panel infrastructure.
  - Expose a tree view showing symbol ↔ doc alignment status, similar to the diagnostics tree.
  - Provide a CLI (under scripts) so CI can run headless drift checks, gating pulls.

- **Workspace convention (MDMD layer‑4)**
  - Add a `docstringBridge` section in our MDMD metadata (front matter or fenced block) specifying the same schema; define it as mandatory for `.mdmd/layer-4/**`.
  - Extend `graph:audit` or a new `graph:docstring-audit` to assert the metadata exists and points to real symbols.
  - Use `slopcop:symbols` to verify headings still match the configured slugs.
  - For our private workflow, maintain a small helper script that hydrates the config from MDMD files (so we edit MDMD, then run a generator that produces the workspace-level config consumed by the extension).

- **Vision & requirements adjustments**
  - Update layer‑1 vision to include “Documentation intelligence must be user-configurable and portable.”
  - Add a layer‑2 requirement around “bidirectional documentation surfaces with workspace-defined conventions,” including configurability guidance.
  - In layer‑3 architecture docs, describe the new bridge module: config resolver, docstring extractor, markdown writer, and diagnostics publisher.

- **Spec tasks**
  1. Define the config schema + docstring metadata format.
  2. Implement shared tooling to read/write docstrings (likely `packages/shared/src/tooling/docstringBridge.ts`).
  3. Add extension UI (commands, tree view, settings entry to locate the config file).
  4. Provide tests/fixtures that demonstrate custom file structures (so non-MDMD users can adopt it).
  5. Document end-to-end usage in layer‑4 MDMD and tooling.

This keeps the extension broadly useful while honoring our MDMD discipline. We can dogfood the system by having `.mdmd/layer-4/**/*.mdmd.md` declare their bridge info, while other users supply their own mappings via the new config file or settings.

jfjordanfarr: Research, in our MDMD and our workspace, what the user surface for configuration looks like versus what tooling we have on offer. Try to determine how to we might take the intelligence that we offer ourselves with MDMD conventions and their guarantees into declarative configurations, with a few preset configs for common setups (MDMD being one such setup). 

GitHub Copilot: Read [](file:///d%3A/Projects/Copilot-Improvement-Experiments/.mdmd/layer-3/extension-surfaces.mdmd.md)

Read [](file:///d%3A/Projects/Copilot-Improvement-Experiments/.mdmd/layer-4/extension-settings/configService.mdmd.md)

Read [](file:///d%3A/Projects/Copilot-Improvement-Experiments/packages/extension/package.json)

Read [](file:///d%3A/Projects/Copilot-Improvement-Experiments/specs/001-link-aware-diagnostics/spec.md)

**Config Surfaces Today**
- Extension: `linkAwareDiagnostics.*` settings (see package.json and configService.mdmd.md) cover provider consent, debounce, noise suppression, hop limits, storage path—purely runtime gating, no declarative doc intelligence.
- Workspace tooling: slopcop.config.json (markdown/symbol/asset audits), graph commands (`npm run graph:snapshot|audit|inspect`) documented in extension-surfaces.mdmd.md and tooling layer-4 docs; these rely on CLI scripts but lack per-project schema for docs-to-code mapping.
- MDMD conventions: layer-4 files encode responsibilities via narrative sections, yet they’re not machine-consumed except indirectly through audits expecting certain headings.
- No shared config bridges the extension UI, CLI audits, and MDMD metadata; everything beyond settings is hardcoded to our workspace assumptions.

**Path to Declarative Doc Intelligence**
- Define a workspace-level config artifact (e.g., `link-aware-docstrings.config.json`) listing `profiles`: each profile names a doc convention (`mdmd-layer4`, `docs/runbooks`, etc.) with `sourceGlobs`, `symbolMatcher`, `markdownTarget`, and required section slugs/front-matter keys.
- Extend extension settings with a pointer to the config file (plus enable/disable toggles per profile) so users can adopt presets or customize paths without editing code.
- Teach CLI/audit tools (`graph:audit`, new `graph:docstrings`) to load the same config: validate doc sections exist, ensure docstrings and markdown stay in sync, emit actionable diagnostics.
- Ship preset configs bundled with the extension: MDMD layer-4, plain `/docs/api/*.md`, JSDoc-style monorepos. Workspace generator chooses a preset, then developers tweak globs.

**Workspace Integration Strategy**
- Embed a `docstringBridge` block in each `.mdmd/layer-4/**` front matter describing the symbol namespace and target sections; add a generator that emits the shared config file from these blocks so our MDMD source remains canonical.
- Update audits to fail when layer-4 docs lack the bridge metadata or when linked symbols are missing docstrings.
- Document the end-to-end flow in layer-3 architecture (new “Documentation Bridge” section) and add spec requirements/tasks for config schema, sync commands, tree view surfacing, and CI enforcement.

**Next Steps**
1. Draft the config schema + MDMD front-matter contract and record it in layer-2 requirements/spec.
2. Add Polish-phase tasks for extractor tooling, extension commands, and CI audit.
3. Prototype the MDMD preset locally, prove the generator produces the config consumed by both extension and scripts, then generalize for other presets.

jfjordanfarr: What other common autodocumentation forumats exist? I feel like csharp has quite a few of these, especially for folks who are trying to get Swagger/OpenAPI working. I know I've built an old .NET Framework 4.8 WebAPI which used Swagger based on docstrings over every public element in the API. It was slick! The autodocumentation was XML, though, and usable primarily by Swagger. Other autodoc formats in other languages I'm not so familiar with, nor am I certain of how much we want our documentation bridging capabilities to have _overlap_ with existing autodoc capabilities. Do we want to live alongside others or be a mutaully exclusive option relative to them? Let's brainstorm and draft up some ideas of how this could work -- what functionalities (especially common configuration setups) we could offer users, and the underlying dials (which users can themselves turn) which make it happen? 

I feel like our current workspace is chock full of exemplary _ingredients_ and no cohesive central _product_, perhaps? 

GitHub Copilot: **Autodoc Landscape**
- **C# / .NET**: XML documentation comments feed Sandcastle, DocFX, and Swashbuckle (Swagger/OpenAPI). Attributes and `[ProducesResponseType]` metadata augment the XML payload.
- **Java / JVM**: Javadoc block comments power HTML docs; Spring REST builds OpenAPI via annotations or SpringDoc config.
- **TypeScript / JS**: TSDoc/JSDoc drive TypeDoc, API Extractor, or Storybook docs; comments often decorated with tags (`@public`, `@remarks`) and emit JSON.
- **Python**: Docstrings formatted with reStructuredText, Google, or NumPy styles, consumed by Sphinx or MkDocs; FastAPI reads type hints + docstrings to publish OpenAPI.
- **Go / Rust**: godoc uses line comments; rustdoc parses `///`/`//!` to render Markdown and generate JSON; both integrate with their package registries.
- **C/C++**: Doxygen parses special comments, can emit HTML/LaTeX/XML/JSON; Sphinx+Breathe pipelines use the XML form.
- **Others**: PHPDoc, Ruby’s YARD, Swift DocC, Haskell Haddock, Kotlin Dokka—each with comment conventions and pluggable renderers.

**Coexist vs. Replace**
- Goal is coexistence: respect established doc outputs while layering cross-file insights.
- Treat our bridge as a synchronizer: it consumes existing doc comments, enriches workspace Markdown (or vice versa), and surfaces drift diagnostics without owning the whole doc stack.
- Provide adapters so teams can keep generating Swagger, Sphinx, or TypeDoc artifacts while benefiting from link-aware checks.

**Config Presets We Could Ship**
- `mdmd-layer4`: enforce `docstringBridge` blocks linking `.mdmd/layer-4/**/*.mdmd.md` to TypeScript/TS docstrings; default for this repo.
- `tsdoc-workspace`: watch `src/**/*.ts`, sync `/** ... */` TSDoc blocks with `/docs/api/*.md`; TypeDoc-compatible tags preserved.
- `python-sphinx`: map `module/*.py` docstrings to `.rst` files; optionally run Sphinx build and parse the inventory to reconcile anchors.
- `dotnet-xml`: consume C# XML docs (via msbuild `.xml` output) and mirror relevant `<summary>`/`<remarks>` into Markdown design docs; allow reverse update by editing Markdown and regenerating XML patches.
- `rustdoc-json`: ingest `rustdoc --output-format json` to validate Markdown references against `summary`/`examples` sections.
- `swagger-sync`: align controller docstrings + Swagger YAML; flag when Markdown contracts drift from OpenAPI source.

**User-Tunable Dials**
- `sources`: globs or tool outputs to read docstrings (code comments, generated XML/JSON).
- `targets`: Markdown/ReST paths plus required section slugs or front-matter keys.
- `direction`: `code→docs`, `docs→code`, or `bidirectional` with conflict-resolution strategy (`preferCode`, `preferDocs`, `merge`).
- `format`: parser selection (TSDoc, reST, XML, godoc) and tag pass-through options.
- `checks`: enable/disable diagnostics for missing docstrings, mismatched headers, or stale examples.
- `automation`: commands to run before/after sync (`typedoc`, `dotnet build /doc`, `sphinx-build`).
- `confidence`: tolerance for auto-generated edits (dry-run vs. apply).

**Extension / Tooling Surface Ideas**
- Settings entry pointing to a shared config file (`linkAwareDocs.config.json`) with profiles; quick pick to select a preset.
- Command palette actions: “Sync Docstrings → Markdown”, “Sync Markdown → Docstrings”, “Preview Drift Report”.
- Diagnostics category “doc-bridge” with quick fixes to insert missing blocks or reconcile differences.
- Tree view grouping symbols by drift status; integrates with existing diagnostics tree.
- CLI `npm run docs:sync -- --profile dotnet-xml --check` for CI enforcement.

**Next Planning Steps**
- Layer-1/2 MDMD update: add vision + requirement for configurable docstring bridges.
- Layer-3 architecture doc describing adapter pipeline (config resolver → parser adapters → sync engine → diagnostics).
- Spec tasks for schema design, adapter implementations (start with TSDoc + XML), extension commands, new audits, and MDMD metadata generator.
- Decide packaging: bundle common presets and document how teams can author custom ones via JSON schema.

This path lets us turn the “ingredients” into a coherent product: configurable doc intelligence that respects existing ecosystems while giving our extension a compelling user-facing capability.

jfjordanfarr: > Layer-1/2 MDMD update: add vision + requirement for configurable docstring bridges.

I'm thinking more broadly about the possibility of documentation with live/hydrated components. In layer 4 MDMD this takes the form of docstring bridges.

In Layer 2? What if we could bridge to Github Issues or Azure DevOps Work Items?

GitHub Copilot: We can generalize the bridging concept across layers so each tier has a “live surface” that can synchronize with external systems:

- **Layer 4 – Implementation Docs**: Docstring bridges remain the canonical pattern—bidirectional sync between code comments and MDMD blocks, with diagnostics highlighting drift. The same model can capture other “live component” snippets (e.g., generated API schemas or CLI output) by extending the bridge metadata to record source commands.

- **Layer 3 – Architecture Layer**: Add a “live references” section describing how the extension ingests or emits structured data. For docstrings it’s the parser/sync pipeline; for upstream trackers we’d outline the issue-link resolver (GitHub/Azure DevOps adapters), data contracts, and diagnostics surfaced when mappings break.

- **Layer 2 – Requirements**: Introduce a new requirement such as “FR-0XX: System MUST synchronize requirement artifacts with external work-item sources.” Details:
  - Support workspace-configured connectors (GitHub Issues, Azure Boards, Jira).
  - Allow MDMD layer-2 docs to declare a canonical issue list (via front matter or inline blocks).
  - Expose drift diagnostics when an issue exists without matching documentation, or docs reference closed/missing items.
  - Provide commands to refresh, acknowledge, or reconcile differences.

- **Layer 1 – Vision**: Expand our mission beyond markdown intelligence to “live documentation” that stays in sync with implementation, planning trackers, and potentially telemetry. This keeps the top-level story cohesive.

- **Spec & Tasks**: Add a polish-phase epic for “Live Documentation Bridges” that covers:
  1. Config schema for external issue providers (token management, query filters, mapping to doc sections).
  2. Shared connector utilities (GitHub GraphQL REST, Azure DevOps REST).
  3. Extension commands to sync work items into MDMD sections and raise diagnostics.
  4. Integration/MDMD updates verifying layer-2 documents declare their bridge metadata.

With this structure, layer-2 documents gain live linkage to issues/work items, layer-3 explains the adapters, and layer-4 houses the docstring bridges. Everything reuses the same bridging config pattern, so users can opt into our doc intelligence while pointing it at their own ecosystems.

jfjordanfarr: ```
- **Spec & Tasks**: Add a polish-phase epic for “Live Documentation Bridges” that covers:
  1. Config schema for external issue providers (token management, query filters, mapping to doc sections).
  2. Shared connector utilities (GitHub GraphQL REST, Azure DevOps REST).
  3. Extension commands to sync work items into MDMD sections and raise diagnostics.
  4. Integration/MDMD updates verifying layer-2 documents declare their bridge metadata.
```

At the same time, I think about the absurd headache of scope creep trying to manage secure enterprise auth with all these providers. We open ourselves up to the world wide web by default and we open ourselves up to trouble trouble trouble. I hesitate. I don't know if Github would be farther than I'd ever want to go. And even then, interop with Github Issues for non-public codebases would be a horrible horrendous nightmare that would absolutely inflate our codebase to cosmic proportions. 

Part of what I love about MDMD and the way that it all works (especially and including our chat history here in the workspace) is that _the workspace is the entire universe_! There is nothing salient to developing this software that we cannot get from inside this workspace! Do I instead want to explore further with workspace-wide intelligence? At the end of the day, that's what I originally signed on to do: I wanted to know, either before or after making a set of changes, in any arbitrary workspace, **what the workspace-wide consequences would be**. 

Live docs is great, but is that core to the mission? You see me arguing with myself here because we're now at about the upper limit of our initial vision. This is a wonderful problem to have but a problem nonetheless. It means thinking through ideas and throwing away many. Spar and self-spar on the vision for what we have, and where we could feasibly take it with just us continuing development, linearly. You can see that we've accomplished a rather gargantuan amount in a rather short period of time. 

GitHub Copilot: We’re hitting the natural edge of what “change-impact inside a workspace” means, so it makes sense to stress-test the vision before we sprawl.

### Core Mission Check
- **Original promise**: given any set of edits, surface the full workspace ripple. Everything we’ve shipped (graph inference, ripple diagnostics, doc drift, SlopCop, benchmarking, Ollama bridge) reinforces that internal guarantee.
- **Workspace as universe**: our current tooling proves we can derive all necessary insight from files, metadata, and runtime artefacts—no external contracts required. That keeps risk low (no auth/secret handling) and makes the system reproducible for any repo.

### Live Docs vs. External Connectors
- **Docstring bridges (layer 4)** feel native: they synchronize two artefacts already in the repo and strengthen cross-layer traceability. This extends the original vision without leaving the workspace.
- **External work-item sync (layer 2)** drifts from the mission. Pulling in GitHub/Azure data drags us into OAuth flows, pagination, offline caching, rate limits, and security reviews. Even if we scoped to PATs, the codepath explosion is massive, and we stop being workspace-first.

### What to Double Down On
1. **Deeper Workspace Intelligence**
   - Improve ripple fidelity: better heuristics, richer AST analysis, smarter hysteresis, ranking/prioritization of diagnostics.
   - Expand docstring bridges and other intra-repo live components (auto-regenerated API schema snapshots, telemetry boil-downs, etc.) that still live in the repo.
   - Continue analytics around graph stability, latency, inference accuracy—our existing pipeline already pays off here.

2. **Extension UX Hardening**
   - Provide more polished surfaces (drift dashboards, quick actions, tree views) that make the workspace insights easier to consume.
   - Offer flexible configurability for doc bridges (presets for TSDoc, Sphinx, Rustdoc) while staying entirely local.

3. **Workspace-Scoped Live Docs**
   - Instead of hitting remote trackers, treat MDMD layer-2 as the source of truth and add derived views (e.g., dynamic task checklists, gap diagnostics when code/files don’t satisfy requirements).
   - Optionally enable “export” commands that serialize our internal state for other systems, but keep ingestion one-way: workspace → external, not vice versa.

### Ideas to Set Aside (for now)
- Full issue tracker integration: we’d lose the all-local simplicity, inflate maintenance, and blur the mission.
- Cloud-backed data stores or remote collaboration features: same reasons.

### Revised Vision Soundbite
“Link-Aware Diagnostics delivers a complete, workspace-local understanding of change impact—across code, documentation, and tooling—so teams can predict and verify consequences without depending on external services.”

From here, we can iterate linearly: finish doc bridges, refine ripple intelligence, polish observability, and keep MDMD orchestration tight. That preserves the focus you originally signed up for and keeps the codebase manageable while still opening new value for users.

jfjordanfarr: >### What to Double Down On
>1. **Deeper Workspace Intelligence**
>   - Improve ripple fidelity: better heuristics, richer AST analysis, smarter hysteresis, ranking/prioritization of diagnostics.

Love this.

> - Expand docstring bridges and other intra-repo live components (auto-regenerated API schema snapshots, telemetry boil-downs, etc.) that still live in the repo.

Love this.

> - Continue analytics around graph stability, latency, inference accuracy—our existing pipeline already pays off here.

Love this too.

> 2. **Extension UX Hardening**
>   - Provide more polished surfaces (drift dashboards, quick actions, tree views) that make the workspace insights easier to consume.

Yes **BUT**. The primary consumer of the outputs of our tooling are LLMs! I think that we need to put some very pioneering focus into returning our results in mutually-human-and-LLM-readable formats like Markdown charts or ASCII art diagrams which SHOW relationships and cause and effect. The ASCII diagrams idea actually has a slight bit of favor on my part, as they appear to be understood even more intuitively than mermaid charts by LLMs (and humans, if shown in plaintext form, and almost certainly the cause is the same).

> - Offer flexible configurability for doc bridges (presets for TSDoc, Sphinx, Rustdoc) while staying entirely local.

100%. I am absolutely onboard with this. 

> 3. **Workspace-Scoped Live Docs**
> - Instead of hitting remote trackers, treat MDMD layer-2 as the source of truth and add derived views (e.g., dynamic task checklists, gap diagnostics when code/files don’t satisfy requirements).

Maybe. Every extension and its dog wants to provide checklists. Let's save this for if we think we need it on layer 2.

> - Optionally enable “export” commands that serialize our internal state for other systems, but keep ingestion one-way: workspace → external, not vice versa.

Eh, if there's a big appetite for it from users, I might get interested, but I think it plausibly won't be necessary or useful enough to warrant the effort investment.

> ### Revised Vision Soundbite
>“Link-Aware Diagnostics delivers a complete, workspace-local understanding of change impact—across code, documentation, and tooling—so teams can predict and verify consequences without depending on external services.”

I'd be okay with changing to this!


GitHub Copilot: Let’s embrace that direction:

- I’ll update the Layer‑1 vision doc (and cross-reference in layer‑2 requirements) to use the new soundbite: **workspace-local change impact across code, docs, tooling—with zero dependency on external services**.

- For extension UX, we’ll prioritize outputs that LLMs can digest: ASCII adjacency diagrams, Markdown tables/charts, and structured JSON payloads for tooling. That can slot into `codeImpact.test.ts` reporting, the diagnostics tree, and future commands (e.g., “Show Ripple Diagram”).

- We’ll keep the docstring bridge plans fully local and configurable (presets for TSDoc, Sphinx, Rustdoc, etc.), but shelve layer‑2 checklist automation and any external export pipelines until there’s clear pull.

- Focus areas moving forward:
  1. **Ripple fidelity** — deeper heuristics, AST integration, smarter hysteresis.
  2. **Doc bridges & live components** — bidirectional sync, plus derived artefacts like schema snapshots or telemetry digests.
  3. **Analytics** — keep enhancing graph stability/latency/accuracy metrics.

If that sounds right, I’ll start drafting the MDMD updates that reframe the vision and note the ASCII-first UX strategy. Let me know if you want specific wording or want me to tackle the documentation pass directly.

jfjordanfarr: That sounds good! Please draft the MDMD edits and #file:001-link-aware-diagnostics spec file(s) edits and I'll review.

Is it wise to bundle markdown generation in an extension which is about workspace intelligence? Is it smarter to simply keep the extension (tentantively called "Link-Aware-Diagnostics" but we probably want a new name) lean and focused on workspace intelligence -- read and no write (except for its little knowledge graph) -- or should we push forward and make this extension able to push harder on workspace intelligence by bridging across different file formats and languages with our pseudocode AST and markdown-to-docstring bridges? GitHub Copilot: Read [](file:///d%3A/Projects/Copilot-Improvement-Experiments/AI-Agent-Workspace/ChatHistory/2025-10-29.md#4578-4578), lines 4578 to 5516

**Review + Sparring**
- Updated docs (link-aware-diagnostics-vision.mdmd.md, product-roadmap.mdmd.md, `specs/001-link-aware-diagnostics/{plan,spec,tasks}.md`) tighten the vision around “workspace-local change impact,” add FR-020/FR-021, introduce Phase 9, and enumerate tasks T079–T086 so docstring bridges and ASCII/Markdown/JSON narratives are now first-class workstreams.
- Runtime additions (localOllamaBridge.ts + test, ollamaClient.ts, index.ts, run-chat.ts, saveCodeChange.ts) harden the local LLM bridge: deterministic fallbacks, context-window overrides, trace capture, CLI parity, and canonical artifact IDs in change logging.

**Should we bundle markdown generation?**
- *Reasons to keep read-mostly*: reinforces the mission (“observe the workspace, don’t mutate it”), reduces risk of clobbering curated docs, keeps the extension simpler to reason about, and still lets CLI tooling or external scripts apply changes when desired.
- *Reasons to allow targeted writes*: docstring/markdown bridges must reconcile drift eventually; offering opt-in write paths (commands/CLI that apply patches) completes the feedback loop and avoids manual copy-paste churn.
- *Middle ground*: default to diagnostics + exportable drafts (Markdown, ASCII diagrams, JSON) while making mutating commands explicit, verbose, and configurable. That preserves trust while enabling power users to close the loop locally.

**Branding explorations**
- **“SlopCop”** – *Elevator pitch*: “An all-in-one workspace fact enforcer for LLM-era development.”  
  Bullet points: workspace-wide lint bundles (markdown/assets/symbols), drift diagnostics with acknowledgement workflow, local LLM bridge for reproducible analyses, ASCII/Markdown change-impact narratives, docstring/markdown bridge framework with presets.
- **“Link-Aware Diagnostics”** – *Pitch*: “Know every file your change touches—instantly, offline.”  
  Bullet points: dependency + documentation graph with hysteresis, ripple diagnostics and acknowledgements, graph audits & benchmarks, local-only LLM assists, exportable ripple reports for humans and copilots.
- **“Workspace Intelligence”** – *Pitch*: “A control tower for repository health and impact.”  
  Bullet points: unified change ledger (code/docs/tools), live telemetry (latency, inference accuracy), configurable doc bridges, ASCII/Markdown status stories, safe-to-commit orchestrations.
- **“SuperLinter”** – *Pitch*: “Beyond lint—enforce every workspace contract.”  
  Bullet points: traditional lint coverage plus graph-aware diagnostics, documentation integrity (slugs/links/docstrings), fixture verification, CLI + CI gates, local model fallbacks.

**What we’re really building**
The plan, spec, and roadmap all anchor on “workspace-local change impact, zero external dependencies.” The future phases (doc bridges, ASCII/Markdown storytelling) deepen that intelligence rather than broadening into generic linting. “Link-Aware Diagnostics” still captures the mission best, with “SlopCop” branding fitting the lint/audit toolchain we expose. If we keep the extension’s default posture read-mostly with optional, explicitly-scoped write commands, we preserve the core value while empowering advanced workflows.

**Suggested next moves**
1. Capture the read-vs-write stance in the Layer‑3 architecture docs and Phase 9 tasks (diagnostics first, optional mutators).
2. Prototype ASCII ripple output (T084/T085) in dry-run mode to validate that the narrative renderer meets the human+LLM goal before wiring any auto-write paths.

jfjordanfarr: > Middle ground: default to diagnostics + exportable drafts (Markdown, ASCII diagrams, JSON) while making mutating commands explicit, verbose, and configurable. That preserves trust while enabling power users to close the loop locally.

I think that the middle ground should be tiered adoption by users. Here in our workspace, we had to build our way up to the correctness and verification we now have. It's pretty remarkable! But it didn't happen all at once. So too may it be for our user experience: At first, we provide you with a fair bit of intelligence (with some false positives/false negatives) but require **no workspace/documentation contract changes** at all. But once the user is comfortable with the shape of their workspace, as we became, and they settle in on their own favorite shape (mine being MDMD, theirs being some other arbitrary shape), we give them the tools to _simply_ and _reliably_ build _additional enforceable contracts and guardrails on their own docs and code_ such that they can reach total AST-backed internal correctness checks like we have -- and, as we shall eventually have -- bidirectional docstring bridges (if it turns out to be a fruitful path for us). 

What would a **tiered** approach to workspace correctness, or a **configured-by-path-scope** approach to workspace correctness look like? How could we help users increaingly refine a collection of workspace paths to ever-increasing mutual correctness (i.e. code root and docs root mutual correctness) by enabling more and more checks against those paths, and more and more bridges (i.e. docstring bridge, markdown link, etc.)? 

I want greenfield projects to fall down the pit of success, starting from a simple prompt and a set of code-and-docs rules that causes linting to drive copilot to inadvertently developing and documenting the entire thing, from pure common-sense. 

I want brownfield projects to get a new chance to have always-up-to-date documentation and documentation-based evidence of program correctness. I want brownfield projects to be able to have a good chunk of their documentation generated procedurally by our tooling if they so request it. 

I want all projects to have total clarify on the effect of any arbitrary change on any other arbitrary workspace file, and with a focus on salient connections between files and symbols. 

GitHub Copilot: **Tiered Adoption Model**  
- Stage 0 (*Observe*): Ship the extension in read-only mode—graph inference, ripple diagnostics, Markdown/ASCII/JSON exports, and audit CLIs enabled; no doc conventions required. Users can scope by glob in settings (“diagnostics:include/exclude”) so brownfields start narrow (e.g., `src/core/**`) while greenfields leave defaults broad.  
- Stage 1 (*Guard*): Let users opt specific path profiles into additional checks (markdown links, symbol slugs, asset paths). Profiles live in a declarative config (`linkAware.workspaceProfiles`). Each profile pairs `sourceGlobs` + `docGlobs` with a checklist of validators (SlopCop suites, graph coverage thresholds). Diagnostics stay informational until the profile’s `enforce` flag flips.  
- Stage 2 (*Bridge*): Profiles gain bridge modules—docstring sync, schema excerpts, telemetry snapshots. Each bridge defines direction (`code→docs`, `docs→code`, `bidirectional`), conflict strategy, and dry-run vs. apply. Commands like `Bridge: Preview Sync` and `Bridge: Apply Profile` are explicit so mutation is conscious.  
- Stage 3 (*Sustain*): Profiles become contracts. `safe:commit`/CI treat failures as blocking, ASCII narratives document impact, and MDMD (or any other format) is regenerated by scripted tasks. Public APIs are now covered by docstring bridges, architecture docs validated against generated graphs.

**Configured-by-Path Refinement**  
- Profiles allow progressive activation: start with `src/runtime/**` + `docs/runtime/**`, later add `packages/api/**`. Each profile can dial coverage (`requireDocstrings`, `requireBenchmarks`, `requireSnapshots`).  
- Bridge adapters plug in per language (TSDoc, XML, reST, rustdoc JSON) so teams pick the format that matches their existing docs.  
- Audits and exports always report by profile, giving instant visibility into which slice has reached “mutual correctness.”

**Greenfield Guidance**  
1. Run `/init link-aware` wizard to scaffold a preset (MDMD, TSDoc, etc.), generate profile config, and seed Layer-4 docs.  
2. Start in Stage 0, flip to Stage 1 when SlopCop passes consistently, then enable bridges once docstring comments settle.  
3. ASCII ripple stories and JSON exports feed Copilot prompts so engineers stay within the “pit of success.”

**Brownfield Ramp**  
- Begin with Observation on a safe subset (core services).  
- Use the profiler report to identify drift hotspots, then enable Guard validators gradually.  
- Offer CLIs (`ripple report --profile legacy`) to produce procedural documentation, letting teams rewrite docs incrementally.  
- Only adopt bridges on modules that already have docstrings; generated Markdown provides a starting point where docs are missing.

**All Projects—Ultimate Goal**  
- Stable change-impact map for any edit, with salient relationships surfaced via ASCII/Markdown narratives and symbol-aware diagnostics.  
- Profiles capture the organization’s chosen contracts; once they reach Stage 3, code + docs behave like a living AST, with Copilot and humans sharing the same authoritative view.

**Next Steps**  
1. Prototype the profile config schema + wizard (Stage 0/1 scaffolding).  
2. Extend Phase 9 tasks so `docstringBridgeService` consumes profile definitions and emits per-profile diagnostics/reports.  
3. Add ASCII/Markdown narrative integration per profile, with toggles for dry-run/apply to respect the tiered cadence.
